            }
            return "blank";
        }

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="ecacaee8b6084a57bd4dfe0883eb3340">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '12569787-3bc9-4b1a-bf7c-0520207b91d8'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en">
         <h2 id="d11005942" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p>A novel concept of “atomic migration” is proposed to explain phase separation and poor reaction reversibility in sodium-ion batteries. Instead of standing still, the atoms can migrate dynamically in carbon layers during discharge, which is tracked by in situ transmission electron microscopy. The atomic migration-induced irreversible reaction that leads to a low initial coulombic efficiency (ICE) and rapid degradation of electrode performance is unraveled.
            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/38a0d8ba-e332-4127-be94-6d87e85ee9e7/anie202303343-toc-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/38a0d8ba-e332-4127-be94-6d87e85ee9e7/anie202303343-toc-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/e1f20ed1-5e2f-4d2c-9541-654cfb793b8d/anie202303343-toc-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/38a0d8ba-e332-4127-be94-6d87e85ee9e7/anie202303343-toc-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d11005945" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>In sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), the low initial coulombic efficiency (ICE) is commonly induced by irreversible phase conversion and difficult desodiation, especially on transition metal compounds (TMCs). Yet the underlying physicochemical mechanism of poor reaction reversibility is still a controversial issue. Herein, by using in situ transmission electron microscopy and in situ X-ray diffraction, we demonstrate the irreversible conversion of NiCoP@C is caused by the rapid migration of P in carbon layer and preferential formation of isolated Na<sub>3</sub>P during discharge. By modifying the carbon coating layer, the migration of Ni/Co/P atoms is inhibited, thus the improvement of ICE and cycle stability is realized. The inhibiting of fast atom migration which induces component separation and rapid performance degradation might be applied to a wide range of electrode materials, and guides the development of advanced SIBs.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202303343-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202303343-sec-0001-title"> Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) emerge as a potential candidate for large-scale energy storage and have become a hot topic in recent years.<span><sup><a href="#anie202303343-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303343-bib-0001_R_d11005934e694" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1">1</a></sup></span> Recently, transition metal compounds (TMCs) have been used as high-performance anodes in SIBs.<span><sup><a href="#anie202303343-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303343-bib-0004_R_d11005934e697" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2">2</a></sup></span> Among these materials, transition metal phosphides (TMPs) usually have a higher specific capacity of 500–2400 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> (MP<sub><i>x</i></sub>+3 <i>x</i> Na→<i>x</i> Na<sub>3</sub>P+M, M=Ni, Fe, Co, etc.).<span><sup><a href="#anie202303343-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303343-bib-0008_R_d11005934e712" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3">3</a></sup></span> Currently, most researchers assume that the TMCs anodes present poor cycle stability and moderate battery performance due to the sluggish Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics and huge volume expansion during repeat sodiation/desodiation.<span><sup><a href="#anie202303343-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303343-bib-0013_R_d11005934e717" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4">4</a></sup></span> Therefore, a variety of strategies have been proposed, including nanostructure engineering,<span><sup><a href="#anie202303343-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303343-bib-0011_R_d11005934e720" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3c - Nano Energy">3c</a>, <a href="#anie202303343-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303343-bib-0016_R_d11005934e720" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Mater.">5</a></sup></span> carbon coating,<span><sup><a href="#anie202303343-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303343-bib-0017_R_d11005934e724" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6">6</a></sup></span> element doping,<span><sup><a href="#anie202303343-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303343-bib-0021_R_d11005934e727" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7">7</a></sup></span> and electrolyte optimization,<span><sup><a href="#anie202303343-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303343-bib-0024_R_d11005934e730" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Energy Environ. Sci.">8</a></sup></span> which can improve the electrochemical performance to a certain extent. Generally, TMPs often need to be compounded with carbon substrate to enhance the conductivity and inhibit the volume expansion of electrode.<span><sup><a href="#anie202303343-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303343-bib-0025_R_d11005934e733" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Small">9</a></sup></span> However, an obvious capacity decay still occurs during the initial cycle, which leads to low initial coulombic efficiency (ICE) and the degradation of energy density in SIBs.<span><sup><a href="#anie202303343-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303343-bib-0026_R_d11005934e736" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10">10</a></sup></span> So far, the TMPs anodes for SIBs exhibit only 30–60 % ICE (Table S1). Therefore, it is vital to identify key factors leading to low ICE and propose corresponding strategies for the commercialization of SIBs.</p>
         
         <p>The factors that cause low ICE can be mainly explained in the following aspects. First, the decomposition of electrolyte and the formation of solid electrolyte interface (SEI) film, which are inevitable in the charge/discharge process, and the SEI layer is expected to stabilize electrode surface in the following cycles.<span><sup><a href="#anie202303343-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303343-bib-0024_R_d11005934e742" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Energy Environ. Sci.">8</a>, <a href="#anie202303343-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303343-bib-0030_R_d11005934e742" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Energy Storage Mater.">11</a></sup></span> Second, the trapping effect of disordered carbon (defects/pores) or surface functional groups.<span><sup><a href="#anie202303343-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303343-bib-0031_R_d11005934e745" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12">12</a></sup></span> It is generally believed that the disordered carbon of substrate can easily capture Li<sup>+</sup>/Na<sup>+</sup>/K<sup>+</sup>, and the deintercalation of Li<sup>+</sup>/Na<sup>+</sup>/K<sup>+</sup> is difficult due to the strong adsorption, thus leading to a low reaction reversibility.<span><sup><a href="#anie202303343-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303343-bib-0034_R_d11005934e761" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13">13</a></sup></span> Third, the poor reversibility of phase transition in conversion-type materials during sodiation/desodiation.<span><sup><a href="#anie202303343-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303343-bib-0037_R_d11005934e764" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Nano Energy">14</a></sup></span> The phase transition process of TMCs can be detected by X-ray diffraction (XRD).<span><sup><a href="#anie202303343-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303343-bib-0038_R_d11005934e767" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Energy Mater.">15</a></sup></span> Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Z (Z=O, S, P, etc.) formed during the sodiation process cannot completely return to the original TMCs in the subsequent cycles.<span><sup><a href="#anie202303343-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303343-bib-0039_R_d11005934e774" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Nano Lett.">16</a></sup></span> However, the underlying mechanism for incomplete conversion of products remains unclear and rare studies focus on the phase transition/separation details at nano/atomic scale and the atom migration of TMCs.</p>
         
         <p>To better understand the factors affecting reaction reversibility and optimize the electrochemical performance, herein we construct a series of NiCoP@C with different disordered carbon contents as anode of SIBs. By using in situ transmission electron microscopy (TEM), the atomic scale structural evolution and product distribution are visualized. Real time observation reveals that different atom migration rates result in the separation of discharge products, thus hinder the subsequent recombination and cause a low ICE. Synchrotron X-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS), X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) and XRD characterization demonstrate the fast migration of P in carbon results in phase separation and irreversible transition of discharge/charge products. Density functional theory (DFT) calculations further confirmed the fast migration of P in carbon compared with Ni and Co. By modifying the coating carbon materials with strong anchoring site, the regulation of atom migration rate can be realized. Thus, achieved a 1.7 times ICE improvement and a specific capacity of 224 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup> after 1500 cycles.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202303343-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202303343-sec-0002-title"> Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>Herein, to explore the materials structure and electrochemical performance relationship of NiCoP@C, a series of NiCoP@C with different phosphating temperatures from 300 to 600 °C are prepared (which are denoted as NiCoP@C−N, N=300–600). The self-supported NiCoP@C nanoneedle arrays grown on Ni foam were constructed by hydrothermal synthesis and phosphorization (Figure S1). Briefly, the nickel-cobalt hydroxide was grown on the Ni foam with a smooth surface. Then, the ordered hierarchical arrays composed of nanoneedles were grown on the surface in urea solution during the second hydrothermal step. Finally, the NiCoP@C retained the nanoneedle morphology of about 2.5 μm after phosphorization is shown in Figure <a href="#anie202303343-fig-0001">1a</a> (300 °C). The energy dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDX) elemental mapping images of NiCoP@C show the uniform distribution of C, P, Ni, and Co elements, further confirming the successful phosphorization (Figure <a href="#anie202303343-fig-0001">1b</a>). The XRD pattern (Figure S2) shows the hexagonal phase NiCoP (JCPDS: 71-2336) with three main diffraction peaks at 40.7, 44.5, and 47.3°, which match well with (111), (201), and (210) planes. The nanostructure of NiCoP@C is investigated by TEM, which shows that the nanoneedles are further composed of small nanoparticles about 22 nm in diameter and these nanoparticles are well coated with a thin carbon layer (Figure <a href="#anie202303343-fig-0001">1c</a>). The HRTEM image taken along the <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/85b80793-3b30-4864-8ce6-6411faf10452/anie202303343-math-0001.png"></span><math></math>
 zone axis shows two set lattice fringes with d-spacing of 2.53 and 2.23 Å, which correspond to the (200) and (111) planes of NiCoP (Figure <a href="#anie202303343-fig-0001">1d</a>). A series of diffraction rings matching well with the (100), (200), (111), (201) and (211) planes of NiCoP are also detected by the selected area electron diffraction (SAED) pattern (Figure S3).
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202303343-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/667f3ac7-ddff-4348-9386-2391f316008c/anie202303343-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/667f3ac7-ddff-4348-9386-2391f316008c/anie202303343-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c9e07772-8b0f-42e6-92dc-4a4a3eac5aa9/anie202303343-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/667f3ac7-ddff-4348-9386-2391f316008c/anie202303343-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202303343-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202303343" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>The morphology and microstructure of NiCoP@C at 300 °C, a) SEM image, b) corresponding elemental mapping images, and c), d) HRTEM images and corresponding FFT patterns are shown in the inset. e) Raman spectra of NiCoP@C with different phosphating temperatures. f), g) Initial galvanostatic discharge/charge profiles of NiCoP@C anode at 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup> and corresponding statistics of ICE.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The carbon structure of these NiCoP@C electrodes is studied by Raman spectra (Figure <a href="#anie202303343-fig-0001">1e</a>). The observed D-band (≈1346 cm<sup>−1</sup>) and G-band (≈1590 cm<sup>−1</sup>) can be ascribed to the disordered/defective (sp<sup>3</sup>) carbon sites and ordered graphitic (sp<sup>2</sup>) carbon, respectively.<span><sup><a href="#anie202303343-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303343-bib-0040_R_d11005934e861" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">17</a></sup></span> The intensity ratio of the D-band and G-band (i.e. <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub>) increases from 0.67 to 1.06 with the phosphating temperature from 300 to 600 °C, which indicates the more disordered carbon content at higher temperature due to the violent collision and etching of PH<sub>3</sub> gas molecules. The electrochemical performances of NiCoP@C with different disordered carbon content as anodes for SIBs are also studied. The discharge-charge profiles of the first cycle show that with the increase of phosphating temperature, the initial discharge capacities are 557, 717, 966, and 1738 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a href="#anie202303343-fig-0001">1f</a>). The statistical result shows the average values of ICE are 37 %, 44 %, 50 %, and 63 %, respectively, with the increase of disordered carbon (Figure <a href="#anie202303343-fig-0001">1g</a>). This result is interesting and conflict with the conventional view that the increase of disordered carbon captures the Na<sup>+</sup> thus degrading the reaction reversibility.</p>
         
         <p>To explain the phenomenon and gain insight into the effect of disordered carbon on reaction reversibility, the structure evolution of NiCoP@C during sodiation/desodiation is further observed by in situ TEM. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202303343-fig-0002">2a</a>, NiCoP@C is placed on a copper wire as the working electrode, and a tungsten wire attached with sodium is used as the counter electrode. The thin Na<sub>2</sub>O layer on the surface of Na metal is acted as the solid electrolyte for Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion. Figure <a href="#anie202303343-fig-0002">2b</a> and Movie S1 show the morphology evolution of NiCoP@C-300 and the dynamic distribution of components during the initial sodiation process. Due to the rapid atom migration, the formation of crack and void occurs in NiCoP, and the carbon skeleton of NiCoP@C-300 expands violently during sodiation and the electrode volume expands from 541 nm to 659 nm (21.8 %) within 75 s. After the sodiation, the broken nanoparticles around 3.3 nm can be observed and porous electrode form at the edge (Figure S4). For comparison, we also observe the initial sodiation/desodiation process of NiCoP@C-600 which contains more disordered carbon (Figure <a href="#anie202303343-fig-0002">2c</a> and Movie S2). In sharp contrast, the structure evolution of NiCoP@C-600 is stable with a negligible change during the sodiation process. Unlike the drastic volume expansion of NiCoP@C-300, the size of electrode is only increasing from 141 nm to 149 nm (6.4 %) within 90 s. It is worth noting that the extraction of Na<sup>+</sup> does not significantly destroy the structure of NiCoP@C-600 electrode and the overall expansion of NiCoP@C-600 electrode after the first cycle is only 3.3 % (Figure S5 and Movie S3). It indicates that the increase of disordered carbon does not obviously affect the intercalation/deintercalation of Na<sup>+</sup>. At the same time, the excellent structural stability and the anchoring of disordered carbon to active components are further demonstrated in subsequent cycles (Figure S6 and Movie S4). During the second to fourth sodiation/desodiation process of NiCoP@C-600, the electrode only expanded by about 2.4 % (Figure S6d). The edge of electrode marked in a white square (Figure <a href="#anie202303343-fig-0002">2c</a>) is further analyzed. The trajectory statistics of electrode edge shows that the thickness of carbon layer increases from 5 to 10.4 nm within 90 s, which only grows slightly during the initial sodiation (Figure <a href="#anie202303343-fig-0002">2e</a> and <a href="#anie202303343-fig-0002">f</a>). After four cycles, the thickness of carbon layer remained at about 13.3 nm (Figure S6e). It can be seen that the NiCoP particle is limited in carbon layer without movement and the width of NiCoP particle decreases slightly during discharge (white square in Figure <a href="#anie202303343-fig-0002">2c</a>). During the charge process, the particle grows a little and the final state is slightly wider than the pristine state (Figure <a href="#anie202303343-fig-0002">2e</a>). More importantly, the FFT pattern in Figure <a href="#anie202303343-fig-0002">2c</a> inset picture shows the coated carbon layer before sodiation is amorphous. After desodiation, we can find a few nanocrystals exist in the carbon layer which is Na<sub>3</sub>P (Figure S7). Combined with the width changes of NiCoP particle mentioned above, these nanocrystals formation is caused by P migrated from the particle during the discharge process. In sharp contrast with the electrode expansion of NiCoP@C-300, rare nanocrystals are found in and outside carbon layer and NiCoP distribution are confined in carbon shell. It demonstrates that the expansion of electrode is mainly caused by the rapid atom migration of material itself, rather than the intercalation/deintercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> (Figure <a href="#anie202303343-fig-0002">2d</a>). The increase of disordered carbon content strengthens the anchoring of active elements (Ni/Co/P atoms), thus benefiting the recombination of components and maintaining the electrode structure during repeated sodiation/desodiation processes (Figure S6f).
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202303343-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f97226f5-a421-4b40-88e8-6266fcc5392a/anie202303343-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/f97226f5-a421-4b40-88e8-6266fcc5392a/anie202303343-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/7ccf35c9-f252-42a1-bb9e-8cb0a46d67d2/anie202303343-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f97226f5-a421-4b40-88e8-6266fcc5392a/anie202303343-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202303343-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202303343" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>In situ TEM characterization of NiCoP@C. a) Schematic illustration shows the in situ TEM device for sodiation/desodiation of NiCoP@C. In situ time-sequent TEM images of b) NiCoP@C-300 and c) NiCoP@C-600 during the initial sodiation and desodiation. d) The statistics of expansion ratio between NiCoP@C-300 and NiCoP@C-600 during sodiation/desodiation. e) The corresponding statistics of the thickness of carbon layer and the width of NiCoP particle marked in (c). f) Corresponding trajectory of the carbon layer marked in (c) at sequential time. g) Ex situ TEM images of NiCoP@C-300 and NiCoP@C-600 at pristine, 0.01 V and 3 V states.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>More ex situ TEM characterizations was conduct to compare the structure of NiCoP@C anodes before and after cycling (Figure <a href="#anie202303343-fig-0002">2g</a>). Comparing with the pristine NiCoP@C-300, it shows that NiCoP particles are fragmented into tinny nanoparticles might caused by the fast migration of atoms through the carbon skeleton. Due to the rapid atom migration, the electrode structure is destroyed and numerous voids are found inside. The pristine NiCoP@C-600 with rich nanopores in carbon skeleton, which is generated by PH<sub>3</sub> etching at higher temperature, is benefit for capturing active elements (Figure <a href="#anie202303343-fig-0002">2g</a> and Figure S8).<span><sup><a href="#anie202303343-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303343-bib-0041_R_d11005934e964" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - ACS Appl. Nano Mater.">18</a></sup></span> It can be seen intuitively that the coating carbon layer of NiCoP@C-600 maintains its structure after charge, atom migration is inhibited and NiCoP particles are confined in the carbon skeleton, which is consistent with the results observed by in situ TEM.</p>
         
         <p>To further clarify the origin of poor reaction reversibility, XAS and in situ XRD was used to investigate the lowest ICE materials of NiCoP@C-300 anode. The initial discharge and charge specific capacities of NiCoP@C-300 measured at 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup> are 488.3 and 186.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, with an ICE of 38.2 % (Figure S9a). It reveals that there is a significant discharge capacity attenuation from the second cycle, which is also consistent with the cyclic voltammetry (CV) test (Figure S9b).<span><sup><a href="#anie202303343-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303343-bib-0042_R_d11005934e974" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19">19</a></sup></span> The high purity CoO and Co foil are used as standard references. The Co K-edge X-ray absorption near-edge structure (XANES) spectra are shown in Figure <a href="#anie202303343-fig-0003">3a</a>. From the energy range of 7705 to 7720 eV, the absorption edge orders are Co foil, discharged, charged, pristine state electrode materials, and CoO, respectively. The absorption edge of the pristine electrode is located near the CoO, indicating the valence state of Co in the NiCoP@C anode is near +2. Compared to the pristine sample, the Co K-edge of 0.01 V discharge state shifts to lower energy, suggesting the formation of Co metal during the discharge process. The Co K-edge of 3 V state is located between the edges of 0.01 V and pristine state, which indicate the existence of partial irreversible reactions. In Co K-edge Fourier transform (FT) extended X-ray absorption fine structure (EXAFS) spectra (Figure <a href="#anie202303343-fig-0003">3b</a>), a distinct peak at 2.17 Å corresponds to the Co−Co bond in Co foil. We find that the peaks around 1.65 Å of pristine and charged electrodes are attributed to Co−P bonds. For the discharged electrode, the peak of Co−P bond decreases, and a broad peak at 2.02 Å is observed, indicating the presence of discharge product Co metal. The broad peak and the shift of Co−Co bond reveal the incomplete reaction. Similar to the results of FT-EXAFS, the coordination states of Co are also confirmed by wavelet transform (WT) EXAFS spectra in both R and K space (Figure S10). Figure <a href="#anie202303343-fig-0003">3c</a> shows the in situ XRD patterns of NiCoP@C-300 anode combined with the galvanostatic discharge-charge curves. The relative intensity changes of NiCoP (300) and Na<sub>3</sub>P (200) peaks are shown in Figure S11. The peak intensity of NiCoP gradually decline, while the relatively weak Na<sub>3</sub>P peak emerge, corresponding to the conversion equation (NiCoP+3 Na→Ni+Co+Na<sub>3</sub>P). However, in the charge process, the relatively strong Na<sub>3</sub>P (200) peak means that there are still residual Na<sub>3</sub>P products that cannot be converted back to NiCoP, indicating poor reaction reversibility and low ICE.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202303343-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/702411ec-306c-4e1e-959e-cd1fc7d1270d/anie202303343-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/702411ec-306c-4e1e-959e-cd1fc7d1270d/anie202303343-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/629e5eb3-8ad0-40db-b60e-bffb4c8f95ce/anie202303343-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/702411ec-306c-4e1e-959e-cd1fc7d1270d/anie202303343-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202303343-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202303343" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>a) Co K-edge XANES spectra of NiCoP@C-300 anodes at pristine, 0.01 V, and 3.0 V states. The spectra of CoO and Co foil are shown for comparison. b) Co K-edge EXAFS spectra. c) In situ XRD patterns of NiCoP@C-300 anode during the initial cycle. Galvanostatic discharge-charge curves at a current density of 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup> within the voltage range of 0.01–3.0 V. d), e) HAADF-STEM images of the NiCoP@C-300 at various states with corresponding elemental mappings and linear scan profiles across the nanoneedles of different elements. f), g) HAADF-STEM images of the NiCoP@C-600 at various states with corresponding elemental mappings and linear scan profiles across the nanoneedles of different elements. h) Initial Coulombic efficiency of NiCoP@C-300 anode at different depths of discharge with a current density of 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>To further investigate the factors causing irreversible reaction, the spatial distribution of components is characterized by ex situ TEM and XPS at different scales. The high-angle annular dark-field scanning TEM (HAADF-STEM) images with corresponding elemental mappings of NiCoP@C-300 anodes before and after cycling are shown in Figure <a href="#anie202303343-fig-0003">3d</a> and Figure <a href="#anie202303343-fig-0003">3e</a>. By comparing the elemental mappings, the elemental signal intensity of pristine electrode shows that the C, P, Ni, and Co elements are uniformly distributed (Figure <a href="#anie202303343-fig-0003">3d</a>). When the electrode is fully discharged to 0.01 V, the signal intensities of C and P increase significantly and the signal intensities of Ni and Co are weak, indicating that P migrate to the surface and there is a phase separation due to the different migration rates of Ni/Co/P atoms (Figure S12). Moreover, the elemental mappings marked in the bottom right indicates that only small partial of them recombine into NiCoP in the charged state, while there are still obvious P residues in other areas (Figure <a href="#anie202303343-fig-0003">3e</a>). The local magnification of discharge state electrode shows that the discharge products are separated from each other in carbon skeleton (Figure S13 and Figure S14). Moreover, HRTEM image shows that the tinny nanoparticles with low contrast at the electrode edge are Na<sub>3</sub>P (Figure S15). The Na<sub>3</sub>P remained on the edge of carbon skeleton cannot recombine with Ni and Co for subsequent charge reaction, resulting in an incomplete reaction and lower ICE (Figure S16–S18). In sharp contrast to the rapid migration of P atoms in NiCoP@C-300, the migration of Ni/Co/P atoms is inhibited and anchored in the carbon shell of NiCoP@C-600 charged state due to the existence of abundant nanopores (Figure <a href="#anie202303343-fig-0003">3f</a>, <a href="#anie202303343-fig-0003">g</a> and Figure S12). The elements distribution of NiCoP@C-300 electrode in a wider range are determined by XPS with Ar-cluster beam etching. The full and high-resolution XPS spectra show the existence of Ni, Co, P, and C elements (Figure S19 and Figure S20). Compared with the pristine sample, the signal intensity of Ni and Co on the electrode surface are very weak when the electrode is fully discharged to 0.01 V. With the etching of Ar ion beam, the Ni and Co signals of electrode bulk phase are detected. While the P element always maintains a strong signal from the surface of electrode to the inside, indicating the rapid migration and surface distribution of P element (Figure S21). In a word, in situ XRD has indicated the irreversible phase conversion of Na<sub>3</sub>P, and XPS spectra further confirm its spatial distribution on the surface of electrode. The ICE of NiCoP@C-300 at different depths of discharge (DoD) is also investigated. The ICE is different with the increase of DoD, in particular, the ICE increases from 27.9 % to 36.3 % when the DoD varies from 20 % to 60 % and then remains almost constant. It indicates that the phase separation and isolated Na<sub>3</sub>P have been formed in the early stage of discharge (Figure <a href="#anie202303343-fig-0003">3h</a>).<span><sup><a href="#anie202303343-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303343-bib-0047_R_d11005934e1058" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Nano Lett.">20</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>DFT calculations are carried out to investigate the formation of phase separation. The simplified graphene model is used to simulate carbon layer at the local atomic scale. First, we compare the difference in migration abilities of Ni, Co, P, and Na atoms on ideal graphene. Through the migration path, we find that a certain energy barrier exists when an atom migrates from above the benzene ring center across the C−C bond to the next stable site. The energy barriers of Ni/Co/Na/P atoms migrating on ideal graphene are 0.14, 0.25, 0.10, and 0.003 eV, respectively (Figure <a href="#anie202303343-fig-0004">4a</a>). Compared with Ni and Co, the migration energy barrier of P on graphene is almost zero, which accounts for the fastest migration of P and then leads to phase separation. The simplified graphene model with one pore is used to simulate the disordered carbon, and the effect of disordered carbon on the migration of atoms is discussed.<span><sup><a href="#anie202303343-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303343-bib-0048_R_d11005934e1066" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">21</a></sup></span> The result shows that the migration of Ni/Co/P atoms on disordered carbon need to overcome strong adsorption, which hinders Ni/Co/P atoms migration. Unexpectedly, disordered carbon has almost no effect on Na migration, showing a migration barrier similar to that on ideal graphene (Figure <a href="#anie202303343-fig-0004">4b</a>). In addition, the strong interaction between disordered carbon and Ni/Co/P atoms is also shown in the 3D charge density difference with obvious charge redistribution (Figure S22).<span><sup><a href="#anie202303343-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303343-bib-0049_R_d11005934e1072" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - ACS Nano">22</a></sup></span> These theoretical calculation results are also consistent with the above experimental characterization results. Therefore, with the increase of disordered carbon, the atom migration is further inhibited and more Ni/Co/P components can be trapped together, which makes the subsequent charge reaction easier and improves the reaction reversibility. A similar conclusion can be obtained from the graphene model with two nanopores and a different migration pathway. Although not all the migration paths and the disordered degree of carbon are considered in this calculation, the above results are sufficient to illustrate the different migration rates of elements on the carbon layer and the anchoring effect of disordered carbon on the migration of Ni/Co/P atoms.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202303343-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/5c87bc01-4b95-444e-9b5b-988727cabbd9/anie202303343-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/5c87bc01-4b95-444e-9b5b-988727cabbd9/anie202303343-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4317242f-578f-4a3d-bcdc-2345d52a93f9/anie202303343-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/5c87bc01-4b95-444e-9b5b-988727cabbd9/anie202303343-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202303343-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202303343" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Migration barrier of Ni, Co, Na, and P atoms on a) ideal graphene, b) graphene with one pore. The inset image shows the migration pathway of atoms on graphene. c) Cycling performances of NiCoP@C at 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. d) Schematic illustration of atom migration ability affected the anode performance of SIBs.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Meanwhile, the long-term cycle ability of NiCoP@C is also tested. Among them, NiCoP@C-600 maintains a discharge capacity of 224 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 1500 cycles at 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, showing excellent cycling stability (Figure <a href="#anie202303343-fig-0004">4c</a>). In contrast, the NiCoP@C-300 electrode shows a rapid decay in cycling performance. The rate performance of NiCoP@C-600 is also studied under various current densities (Figure S23). At the current densities of 0.1, 0.2, 0.3, 0.5, 0.8, and 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, NiCoP@C-600 delivers discharge capacities of 1548, 676, 591, 525, 448, and 385 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Moreover, when the current density changes back to 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the discharge capacity could return to 390 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. The reversible rate performance of the NiCoP@C-600 should be attributed to the coating layer with more disordered carbon which limits the migration of Ni/Co/P components and helps to maintain the electrode structure. The electrochemical kinetic property and pseudocapacitive contribution of the NiCoP@C-600 electrode are studied by CV test at different scan rates from 0.1 to 1 mV s<sup>−1</sup> (Figure S24). The b values of NiCoP@C-600 are 0.82 and 0.89 for oxidation and reduction, respectively, indicating that the pseudocapacitive process dominates the storage behavior. Besides, the ratios of capacitive contributions are 66 %, 74 %, 79 %, 82 %, and 87 % at 0.1, 0.3, 0.5, 0.7, and 1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. This capacitive-dominated feature is favorable for fast electrochemical kinetic.<span><sup><a href="#anie202303343-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202303343-bib-0050_R_d11005934e1124" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23">23</a></sup></span> Such a performance of NiCoP@C-600 is also superior to that of other TMPs in SIBs (Figure S25). The schematic illustration of the discharge and charge processes of NiCoP@C is shown in Figure <a href="#anie202303343-fig-0004">4d</a>. During discharge, NiCoP crumbles into numerous fine nanoparticles, and migrate through the carbon skeleton. Due to the faster migration rate of P atoms, part of Na<sub>3</sub>P is isolated at the edge of carbon skeleton which is difficult to recombine with Ni/Co in the subsequent reaction. The carbon skeleton with abundant disordered carbon can inhibit the migration of components, thus improving the electrochemical performance of anodes.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202303343-sec-0003">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202303343-sec-0003-title"> Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In this work, we construct a carbon coated NiCoP@C anode for SIBs. By using in situ TEM, we visualize the effect of disordered carbon on electrode structure evolution during the reaction. Different from previous views, we have demonstrated that the increase of disordered carbon will not affect the removal of Na. On the contrary, the more disordered carbon can inhibit the atom migration of active elements (Ni/Co/P atoms) and improve the reaction reversibility. Combined with EXAFS, in situ XRD, and XPS results, the phase separation of discharge products Na<sub>3</sub>P should be the origin of low ICE and poor battery performance. DFT calculations further reveal that P requires the lowest migration energy barrier compared with Ni and Co, which causes the isolated distribution of Na<sub>3</sub>P. Finally, through the restriction on atom migration by disordered carbon, up to 1.7 times ICE improvement and a specific capacity of 224 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup> after 1500 cycles are achieved. These findings provide new insight into the atom migration-induced irreversible reaction and rapid degradation of electrode performance. Understanding the fundamental mechanism of atom migration regulation is helpful for building advanced energy storage systems.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">

        <script type="text/javascript" nonce="ecacaee8b6084a57bd4dfe0883eb3340">
    window.addEventListener('load', () => {
        document.querySelectorAll('.admin-bar-popup-menu ul li')?.forEach(function (li) {
            const anchor = li.querySelector('a');
            if (anchor && anchor.textContent.trim() === 'Edit Access Denial') {
                li.style.display = 'none';
            }
        });
